<p><b>James Bryant Conant</b> (March 26, 1893&#160;â February 11, 1978) was an American <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a>, a transformative <a href="/wiki/President_of_Harvard_University" title="President of Harvard University">President</a> of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, and the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_West_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Ambassador to West Germany">U.S. Ambassador to West Germany</a>. Conant obtained a PhD in Chemistry from Harvard in 1916. During World War I he served in the U.S. Army, working on the development of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">poison gases</a>. He became an assistant professor of chemistry at Harvard in 1919, and the Sheldon Emery Professor of Organic Chemistry in 1929. He researched the physical structures of <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>, and he was one of the first to explore the sometimes complex relationship between <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">chemical equilibrium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Reaction_rate" title="Reaction rate">reaction rate</a> of chemical processes. He studied the <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oxyhemoglobin" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxyhemoglobin">oxyhemoglobin</a> providing insight into the disease <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobinemia" title="Methemoglobinemia">methemoglobinemia</a>, helped to explain the structure of <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>, and contributed important insights that underlie modern theories of <a href="/wiki/Acid-base_chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid-base chemistry">acid-base chemistry</a>.</p>
<p>In 1933, Conant became the President of Harvard University with a reformist agenda that involved dispensing with a number of customs, including class rankings and the requirement for Latin classes. He abolished <a href="/wiki/Athletic_scholarships" class="mw-redirect" title="Athletic scholarships">athletic scholarships</a>, and instituted an "<a href="/wiki/Up_or_out" title="Up or out">up or out</a>" policy, under which scholars who were not promoted were terminated. His egalitarian vision of education required a diversified student body, and he promoted the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Scholastic_Aptitude_Test" class="mw-redirect" title="Scholastic Aptitude Test">Scholastic Aptitude Test (SAT)</a> and co-educational classes. During his presidency, women were admitted to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Medical_School" title="Harvard Medical School">Harvard Medical School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a> for the first time.</p>
<p>Conant was appointed to the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Research_Committee" title="National Defense Research Committee">National Defense Research Committee</a> (NDRC) in 1940, becoming its chairman in 1941. In this capacity, he oversaw vital wartime research projects, including the development of synthetic rubber, and the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, which developed the first <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bombs</a>. On July 16, 1945, he was among the dignitaries present at the <a href="/wiki/Holloman_Air_Force_Base" title="Holloman Air Force Base">Alamogordo Bombing and Gunnery Range</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity nuclear test</a>, the first detonation of an atomic bomb, and was part of the <a href="/wiki/Interim_Committee" title="Interim Committee">Interim Committee</a> that advised President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> to use atomic bombs on Japan. After the war, he served on the Joint Research and Development Board (JRDC) that was established to coordinate burgeoning defense research, and on the influential General Advisory Committee (GAC) of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> (AEC). While in this position, he would advise the president against starting a development program for the "<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>".</p>
<p>In his later years at Harvard, Conant taught undergraduate courses on the <a href="/wiki/History_and_philosophy_of_science" title="History and philosophy of science">history and philosophy of science</a>, and wrote books explaining the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to laymen. In 1953 he retired as President of Harvard and became the <a href="/wiki/Allied_High_Commission" title="Allied High Commission">United States High Commissioner</a> for Germany, overseeing the restoration of German sovereignty after World War II, and then was Ambassador to West Germany until 1957. On returning to the United States, he criticized the education system in works such as <i>The American High School Today</i> (1959), <i>Slums and Suburbs</i> (1961) and <i>The Education of American Teachers</i> (1963). Between 1965 and 1969, Conant, suffering from a heart condition, worked on his autobiography, <i>My Several Lives</i> (1970). He became increasingly infirm, suffered a series of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a> in 1977, and died in a nursing home the following year.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>James Bryant Conant was born in <a href="/wiki/Dorchester,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorchester, Massachusetts">Dorchester, Massachusetts</a>, on March 26, 1893, the third child and only son of James Scott Conant, a <a href="/wiki/Photoengraver" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoengraver">photoengraver</a>, and his wife Jennett Orr nÃ©e Bryant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198391.E2.80.9392_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198391.E2.80.9392-2">[2]</a></sup> Conant was one of 35 boys who passed the competitive admission exam for the <a href="/wiki/Roxbury_Latin_School" title="Roxbury Latin School">Roxbury Latin School</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Roxbury,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="West Roxbury, Massachusetts">West Roxbury</a> in 1904. He graduated near the top of his class in 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199317.E2.80.9318_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199317.E2.80.9318-3">[3]</a></sup> Encouraged by his science teacher, Newton H. Black, in September of that year he entered <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199320_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199320-4">[4]</a></sup> where he studied <a href="/wiki/Physical_chemistry" title="Physical chemistry">physical chemistry</a> under <a href="/wiki/Theodore_W._Richards" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodore W. Richards">Theodore W. Richards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> under <a href="/w/index.php?title=Elmer_P._Kohler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Elmer P. Kohler (page does not exist)">Elmer P. Kohler</a>. He was also an editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">The Harvard Crimson</a></i>. He joined the <a href="/wiki/Signet_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Signet Society">Signet Society</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delta_Upsilon" title="Delta Upsilon">Delta Upsilon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199327.E2.80.9331_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199327.E2.80.9331-5">[5]</a></sup> and was initiated as a brother of the Omicron Chapter of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Chi_Sigma" title="Alpha Chi Sigma">Alpha Chi Sigma</a> in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-alpha_chi_sigma_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alpha_chi_sigma-6">[6]</a></sup> He graduated <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" class="mw-redirect" title="Phi Beta Kappa">Phi Beta Kappa</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> in June 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199327.E2.80.9331_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199327.E2.80.9331-5">[5]</a></sup> He then went to work on his doctorate, which was an unusual double dissertation. The first part, supervised by Richards, concerned "The Electrochemical Behavior of Liquid Sodium Amalgams"; the second, supervised by Kohler, was "A Study of Certain <a href="/wiki/Cyclopropane" title="Cyclopropane">Cyclopropane</a> Derivatives".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200386_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200386-7">[7]</a></sup> Harvard awarded Conant his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Doctor of Philosophy</a> degree in 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1915, Conant entered into a business partnership with two other Harvard chemistry graduates, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Pennock" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanley Pennock">Stanley Pennock</a> and Chauncey Loomis, to form the LPC Laboratories. They opened a plant in a one-story building in <a href="/wiki/Queens,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Queens, New York">Queens, New York</a>, where they manufactured chemicals used by the pharmaceutical industry like <a href="/wiki/Benzoic_acid" title="Benzoic acid">benzoic acid</a> that were selling at high prices on account of the interruption of imports from Germany due to <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. In 1916, the departure of organic chemist <a href="/wiki/Roger_Adams" title="Roger Adams">Roger Adams</a> created a vacancy at Harvard that was offered to Conant. Since he aspired to an academic career, Conant accepted the offer and returned to Harvard. On November 27, 1916, there was an explosion at the plant. Pennock and two other workers were killed and the plant was completely destroyed. One of the contributing causes was Conant's faulty test procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199338.E2.80.9339_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199338.E2.80.9339-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant197044.E2.80.9345_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant197044.E2.80.9345-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/United_States_declaration_of_war_on_Germany_(1917)" title="United States declaration of war on Germany (1917)">United States declaration of war on Germany</a>, Conant was commissioned as a <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a> in the U.S. Army Sanitary Corps on September 22, 1917. He went to the <a href="/wiki/Camp_American_University" title="Camp American University">Camp American University</a>, where he worked on the development of <a href="/wiki/Poison_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Poison gas">poison gases</a>. Initially, his work concentrated on <a href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustard gas">mustard gas</a>, but in May 1918 Conant took charge of a unit concerned with the development of <a href="/wiki/Lewisite" title="Lewisite">lewisite</a>. He was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Major" title="Major">major</a> on July 20, 1918. A pilot plant was built, and then a full-scale production plant in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, but the war ended before lewisite could be used in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199344.E2.80.9348_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199344.E2.80.9348-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the war ended, Conant was discharged from the Army and returned to Harvard, where he was appointed an assistant professor of chemistry on September 1, 1919. The following year he became engaged to Richards's daughter, Grace (Patty) Thayer Richards. They were married in the <a href="/wiki/Appleton_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="Appleton Chapel">Appleton Chapel</a> at Harvard on April 17, 1920, and had two sons, James Richards Conant, born in May 1923, and Theodore Richards Conant, born in July 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199350.E2.80.9353_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199350.E2.80.9353-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_professor">Chemistry professor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chemistry professor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Conant became an associate professor in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-aip_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199355_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199355-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1925, he visited Germany, then the heart of chemical research,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant197030_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant197030-15">[15]</a></sup> for eight months. He toured the major universities and laboratories there and met many of the leading chemists, including <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Curtius" title="Theodor Curtius">Theodor Curtius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz_Fajans" title="Kazimierz Fajans">Kazimierz Fajans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Fischer" title="Hans Fischer">Hans Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Hantzsch" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Hantzsch">Arthur Hantzsch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Meerwein" title="Hans Meerwein">Hans Meerwein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Meisenheimer" title="Jakob Meisenheimer">Jakob Meisenheimer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Staudinger" title="Hermann Staudinger">Hermann Staudinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Windaus" title="Adolf Windaus">Adolf Windaus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ziegler" title="Karl Ziegler">Karl Ziegler</a>. After Conant returned to the United States, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Amos_Noyes" title="Arthur Amos Noyes">Arthur Amos Noyes</a> made him an attractive offer to move to <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a>. The President of Harvard, <a href="/wiki/Abbott_Lawrence_Lowell" title="Abbott Lawrence Lowell">Abbott Lawrence Lowell</a>, made a counter offer: immediate promotion to professor, effective September 1, 1927, with a salary of $7,000 (roughly equivalent to <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>96,511 as of 2017<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-16">[16]</a></sup>) and a grant of $9,000 per annum for research. Conant accepted and stayed at Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1929, he became the Sheldon Emery Professor of Organic Chemistry, and then, in 1931, the Chairman of the Chemistry Department.<sup id="cite_ref-aip_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1928 and 1933, Conant published 55 papers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390-17">[17]</a></sup> Much of his research, like his double thesis, combined <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural product</a> chemistry with <a href="/wiki/Physical_organic_chemistry" title="Physical organic chemistry">physical organic chemistry</a>. Based on his exploration of <a href="/wiki/Reaction_rate" title="Reaction rate">reaction rates</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">chemical equilibria</a>, Conant was one of the first to recognise that the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">kinetics</a> of these systems is sometimes straightforward and simple, yet quite complex in other cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397-18">[18]</a></sup> Conant studied the effect of <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">haloalkane</a> structure on the rate of <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution</a> with inorganic <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> which, together with earlier work,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> led to what is now known as either the Conant-Finkelstein reaction or more commonly simply the <a href="/wiki/Finkelstein_reaction" title="Finkelstein reaction">Finkelstein reaction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A recent application of this reaction involved the preparation of an iodinated <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_chloride" title="Polyvinyl chloride">polyvinyl chloride</a> from regular PVC.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> A combination of Conant's work on the kinetics of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Kistiakowsky" title="George Kistiakowsky">George Kistiakowsky</a>'s work on the <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Enthalpy change">enthalpy changes</a> of these reactions<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> supported the later development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Hyperconjugation" title="Hyperconjugation">hyperconjugation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979213_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979213-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conant's investigations helped in the development of a more comprehensive understanding of the nature of acids and bases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979212_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979212-25">[25]</a></sup> He investigated the properties of certain <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> which were many times <a href="/wiki/Acid_strength" title="Acid strength">stronger</a> than <a href="/wiki/Mineral_acid" title="Mineral acid">mineral acid</a> solutions in water. Conant christened them "<a href="/wiki/Superacid" title="Superacid">superacids</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallConant19273047_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallConant19273047-26">[26]</a></sup> and laid the foundation for the development of the <a href="/wiki/Hammett_acidity_function" title="Hammett acidity function">Hammett acidity function</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These investigations used <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> and demonstrated that <a href="/wiki/Sodium_acetate" title="Sodium acetate">sodium acetate</a> behaves as a <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> under these conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This observation is consistent with <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="BrÃ¸nstedâLowry acidâbase theory">BrÃ¸nstedâLowry acidâbase theory</a> (published in 1923)<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> but cannot be explained under older <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction#Arrhenius_definition" title="Acidâbase reaction">Arrhenius theory approaches</a>. Later work with George Wheland<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and extended by William Kirk McEwen<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> looked at the properties of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> as very weak acids, including <a href="/wiki/Acetophenone" title="Acetophenone">acetophenone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenylacetylene" title="Phenylacetylene">phenylacetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorene" title="Fluorene">fluorene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diphenylmethane" title="Diphenylmethane">diphenylmethane</a>. Conant can be considered alongside <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Nicolaus_Br%C3%B8nsted" title="Johannes Nicolaus BrÃ¸nsted">BrÃ¸nsted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Martin_Lowry" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Martin Lowry">Lowry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Lewis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Plack_Hammett" title="Louis Plack Hammett">Hammett</a> as a developer of modern understanding of acids and bases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979212-213_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979212-213-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1929 and his retirement from chemical research in 1933,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979214_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979214-34">[34]</a></sup> Conant published papers in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_American_Chemical_Society" title="Journal of the American Chemical Society">Journal of the American Chemical Society</a></i> about <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> and its structure.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979213_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979213-24">[24]</a></sup> Though the complete structure eluded him, his work did support and contribute to <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Chemistry" title="List of Nobel laureates in Chemistry">Nobel laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Fischer" title="Hans Fischer">Hans Fischer</a>'s ultimate determination of the structure in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Conant's work on chlorophyll was recognised when he was inducted as a foreign <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow of the Royal Society</a><sup id="cite_ref-frs_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-1">[1]</a></sup> on May 2, 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> He also published three papers describing the <a href="/wiki/Polymerisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Polymerisation">polymerisation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isoprene" title="Isoprene">isoprene</a> to prepare <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_rubber" title="Synthetic rubber">synthetic rubber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another line of research involved the <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin-oxyhemoglobin</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198394.E2.80.9397-18">[18]</a></sup> Conant ran a series of experiments with <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemical</a> oxidation and reduction, following in the footsteps of the famous German chemist and Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Haber" title="Fritz Haber">Fritz Haber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant197060_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant197060-41">[41]</a></sup> He determined that the <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> centre in <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobin" title="Methemoglobin">methemoglobin</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Ferric" title="Ferric">ferric</a> (Fe<sup>III</sup>) centre, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Ferrous" title="Ferrous">ferrous</a> (Fe<sup>II</sup>) centre found in normal hemoglobin,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and this difference in <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> is the cause of <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobinemia" title="Methemoglobinemia">methemoglobinemia</a>, a medical condition which causes <a href="/wiki/Tissue_hypoxia" class="mw-redirect" title="Tissue hypoxia">tissue hypoxia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conant wrote a chemistry textbook with his former science teacher Black, entitled <i>Practical Chemistry</i>, which was published in 1920, with a revised edition in 1929. This was superseded in 1937 by their <i>New Practical Chemistry</i>, which in turn had a revised edition in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983121.E2.80.93124_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983121.E2.80.93124-45">[45]</a></sup> The text proved a popular one; it was adopted by 75 universities, and Conant received thousands of dollars in <a href="/wiki/Royalties" class="mw-redirect" title="Royalties">royalties</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200393_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200393-46">[46]</a></sup> For his accomplishments in chemistry, he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a>'s Nichols Medal in 1932,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200393_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200393-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>'s Chandler Medal in 1932,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and the American Chemical Society's highest honor, the <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Medal" title="Priestley Medal">Priestley Medal</a>, in 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> He was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1924,<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-49">[49]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-aip_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip-13">[13]</a></sup> Notable students of Conant's included <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bartlett" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul Bartlett">Paul Bartlett</a>, George Wheland, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Westheimer" title="Frank Westheimer">Frank Westheimer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaltzman200389.E2.80.9390-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1932 he was also honored by membership of the <a href="/wiki/German_Academy_of_Sciences_Leopoldina" class="mw-redirect" title="German Academy of Sciences Leopoldina">German Academy of Sciences Leopoldina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>After some months of lobbying and discussion, Harvard's ruling body, the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard Corporation">Harvard Corporation</a>, announced on May 8, 1933, that it had elected Conant as the next President of Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199370.E2.80.9375_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199370.E2.80.9375-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a>, Harvard's eminent professor of philosophy disagreed with the decision, declaring:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Corporation should not have elected a chemist to the Presidency."<br />
"But," Corporation member <a href="/wiki/Grenville_Clark" title="Grenville Clark">Grenville Clark</a> reminded him, "<a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Eliot" title="Charles William Eliot">Eliot</a> was a chemist, and our best president too."<br />
"I know," replied Whitehead "but Eliot was a <i>bad</i> chemist."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199375_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199375-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Clark was very much responsible for Conant's election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHill201485-86_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHill201485-86-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="President_of_Harvard">President of Harvard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: President of Harvard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On October 9, 1933, Conant became the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Harvard_University" title="President of Harvard University">President of Harvard University</a> with a low-key installation ceremony in the Faculty Room of <a href="/wiki/University_Hall_(Harvard_University)" title="University Hall (Harvard University)">University Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-prez_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prez-54">[54]</a></sup> This set the tone for Conant's presidency as one of informality and reform. At his inauguration he accepted the charter and seal presented to <a href="/wiki/John_Leverett_the_Younger" title="John Leverett the Younger">John Leverett the Younger</a> in 1707, but dropped a number of other customs, including the singing of <i><a href="/wiki/Gloria_Patri" title="Gloria Patri">Gloria Patri</a></i> and the Latin Oration. This was a sign of things to come.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199377_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199377-55">[55]</a></sup> While, unlike some other universities, Harvard did not require Greek or Latin for entrance, they were worth double credits towards admission, and students like Conant who had studied Latin were awarded an <a href="/wiki/Artium_Baccalaureus" class="mw-redirect" title="Artium Baccalaureus">A.B.</a> degree while those who had not, received an <a href="/wiki/Scienti%C3%A6_Baccalaureus" class="mw-redirect" title="ScientiÃ¦ Baccalaureus">S.B</a>. One of his first efforts at reform was to attempt to abolish this distinction, which took over a decade to accomplish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970419.E2.80.93420_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970419.E2.80.93420-56">[56]</a></sup> But in 1937 he wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I do not see how one can make very much headway as a student&#160;... of history and literature without a reading knowledge of Latin. I do not see how a person can go very far in any branch of science without a thorough understanding of mathematics, and if the underpinning was bad in school, probably the necessary <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and so forth would not have been taken during the college years. I know that a man cannot be a research chemist without a reading knowledge of German. It is hard to acquire it as the first language in college.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970189_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970189-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Other reforms included the abolition of class rankings and <a href="/wiki/Athletic_scholarships" class="mw-redirect" title="Athletic scholarships">athletic scholarships</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398-58">[58]</a></sup> but his first, longest and most bitter battle was over <a href="/wiki/Tenure_(academic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenure (academic)">tenure</a> reform, shifting to an "<a href="/wiki/Up_or_out" title="Up or out">up or out</a>" policy, under which scholars who were not promoted were terminated. A small number of extra-departmental positions was set aside for outstanding scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398-58">[58]</a></sup> This policy led junior faculty to revolt, and nearly resulted in Conant's dismissal in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983107.E2.80.93109_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983107.E2.80.93109-59">[59]</a></sup> Conant was fond of saying: "Behold the turtle. It makes progress only when it sticks its neck out."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199389_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199389-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thirteen men pose for a formal picture. Seven are sitting and six standing. There are all wearing either suits or academic gowns, except Bradley, who is in his Army uniform with a garrison cap." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg/300px-Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg" width="300" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg/450px-Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg/600px-Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="832" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oppenheimer_Marshall_Conant_Bradley_and_others_at_Harvard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Award of <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degrees</a> at Harvard to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Oppenheimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Oppenheimer">Robert Oppenheimer</a> (left), <a href="/wiki/George_C._Marshall" class="mw-redirect" title="George C. Marshall">George C. Marshall</a> (third from left) and <a href="/wiki/Omar_N._Bradley" class="mw-redirect" title="Omar N. Bradley">Omar N. Bradley</a> (fifth from left) in June 1947. Conant sits between Marshall and Bradley. Marshall used the occasion to announce the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Conant added new graduate degrees in education, history of science and public policy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199379_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199379-61">[61]</a></sup> and he introduced the <a href="/wiki/Nieman_Fellowship" title="Nieman Fellowship">Nieman Fellowship</a> for journalists to study at Harvard,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970399.E2.80.93402_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970399.E2.80.93402-62">[62]</a></sup> the first of which was awarded in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> He supported the "meatballs", as <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">lower class</a> students were called.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380-64">[64]</a></sup> He instituted the Harvard national scholarships for underprivileged students.<sup id="cite_ref-prez_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prez-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dudley_House_(Harvard_College)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dudley House (Harvard College)">Dudley House</a> was opened as a place where non-resident students could stay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380-64">[64]</a></sup> Conant asked two of his assistant deans, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Chauncey" title="Henry Chauncey">Henry Chauncey</a> and William Bender, to determine whether the <a href="/wiki/Scholastic_Aptitude_Test" class="mw-redirect" title="Scholastic Aptitude Test">Scholastic Aptitude Test (SAT)</a> was a good measure of academic potential. When they reported that it was, Conant adopted it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199381_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199381-65">[65]</a></sup> He waged a ten-year campaign for the consolidation of testing services, which resulted in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Educational_Testing_Service" title="Educational Testing Service">Educational Testing Service</a> in 1946, with Chauncey as its director.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993707_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993707-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theodore_H._White" title="Theodore H. White">Theodore H. White</a>, a Boston Jewish meatball who received a personal letter of introduction from Conant so that he could report on the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>, noted that "Conant was the first president to recognize that meatballs were Harvard men too."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Instead of conducting separate but identical undergraduate courses for Harvard students and students from <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, Conant instituted co-educational classes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett198398-58">[58]</a></sup> It was during his presidency that the first class of women were admitted to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Medical_School" title="Harvard Medical School">Harvard Medical School</a> in 1945, and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a> in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Lowell, Conant's predecessor, had imposed a 15 percent quota on Jewish students in 1922, something Conant had voted to support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199358.E2.80.9359_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199358.E2.80.9359-68">[68]</a></sup> This quota was later substituted with geographic distribution preferences, having the same effect of limiting Jewish admission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199381.E2.80.9383_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199381.E2.80.9383-69">[69]</a></sup> In the words of historians Morton and Phyllis Keller, "Conant's pro-quota position in the early 1920s, his preference for more students from small towns and cities and the South and West, and his cool response to the plight of the Jewish academic refugees from Hitler suggest that he shared the mild antisemitism common to his social group and time. But his commitment to <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a> made him more ready to accept able Jews as students and faculty."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellerKeller200149_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellerKeller200149-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1934, Harvard-educated German businessman <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hanfstaengl" title="Ernst Hanfstaengl">Ernst Hanfstaengl</a> attended the 25th anniversary reunion of his class of 1909, and gave a number of speeches, including the 1934 <a href="/wiki/Commencement_address" class="mw-redirect" title="Commencement address">commencement address</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199380-64">[64]</a></sup> Hanfstaengl wrote out a check for 2,500 <a href="/wiki/Reichsmark" title="Reichsmark">Reichsmarks</a> (roughly equivalent to <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>187,981 as of 2017) to Conant for a scholarship to enable an outstanding Harvard student to study for a year in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970142_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970142-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Harvard_College" title="President and Fellows of Harvard College">President and Fellows of Harvard College</a> rejected the offer due to Hanfstaengl's Nazi associations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970144_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970144-73">[73]</a></sup> When the issue of Hanfstaengl's scholarship came up again in 1936, Conant turned the money down a second time.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Hanfstaengl's presence on campus prompted a series of anti-Nazi demonstrations, in which a number of Harvard and <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> students were arrested. Conant made a personal plea for clemency that resulted in two girls being acquitted, but six boys and a girl were sentenced to six months in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a> awarded an <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degree</a> in 1934 to American legal scholar and Dean of Harvard Law School <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Pound" title="Roscoe Pound">Roscoe Pound</a>, who had toured Germany earlier that year and made no secret of his admiration for the Nazi regime,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Conant refused to order Pound not to accept it, and attended the informal award ceremony at Harvard where Pound was presented with the degree by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Luther" title="Hans Luther">Hans Luther</a>, the German ambassador to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199387_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199387-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> While Conant declined to participate in the Emergency Committee in Aid of Displaced German Scholars,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199384_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199384-79">[79]</a></sup> Harvard awarded honorary degrees to two notable displaced scholars, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199388_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199388-80">[80]</a></sup> What Conant feared most was disruption to Harvard's tercentennial celebrations in 1936, but there was no trouble, despite the presence of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, and a Harvard graduate of the class of 1904, whom many Harvard graduates regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Class_traitor" title="Class traitor">class traitor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg199390.E2.80.9391_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg199390.E2.80.9391-81">[81]</a></sup> It was only with difficulty that Lowell was persuaded to be presiding officer at an event at which Roosevelt spoke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970153_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970153-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>An incident took place during the <a href="/wiki/1941_Harvard%E2%80%93Navy_lacrosse_game" title="1941 HarvardâNavy lacrosse game">1941 HarvardâNavy lacrosse game</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Harvard Crimson men's lacrosse">Harvard Crimson men's lacrosse</a> team attempted to field a player of <a href="/wiki/African-Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="African-Americans">African-American descent</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Navy_Midshipmen_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Navy Midshipmen men's lacrosse">Navy Midshipmen men's lacrosse</a> team's coach then refused to field his team. Harvard's athletic director, William J. Bingham, overruled his lacrosse coach, and had the player, Lucien Victor Alexis, Jr., sent back to Cambridge on a train. Conant subsequently apologized to the <a href="/wiki/Commandant_of_Midshipmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Commandant of Midshipmen">Commandant of Midshipmen</a>. After serving in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Alexis was refused admittance to Harvard Medical School on the grounds that, as the only black student, he would have no one to room with. Alexis graduated from Harvard Business School instead.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Defense_Research_Committee">National Defense Research Committee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: National Defense Research Committee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In June 1940, with World War II already raging in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a>, the director of the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institution_of_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnegie Institution of Washington">Carnegie Institution of Washington</a>, recruited Conant to the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Research_Committee" title="National Defense Research Committee">National Defense Research Committee</a> (NDRC),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993126_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993126-84">[84]</a></sup> although he remained president of Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart194826_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart194826-85">[85]</a></sup> Bush envisaged the NDRC as bringing scientists together to ""conduct research for the creation and improvement of instrumentalities, methods and materials of warfare."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart19488_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart19488-86">[86]</a></sup> Although the United States had not yet entered the war, Conant was not alone in his conviction that <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> had to be stopped, and that the United States would inevitably become embroiled in the conflict. The immediate task, as Conant saw it, was therefore to organize American science for war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993126_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993126-84">[84]</a></sup> He became head of the NDRC's Division B, the division responsible for bombs, fuels, gases and chemicals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart194810_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart194810-87">[87]</a></sup> On June 28, 1941, Roosevelt signed Executive Order 8807, which created the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Scientific_Research_and_Development" title="Office of Scientific Research and Development">Office of Scientific Research and Development</a> (OSRD),<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> with Bush as its director. Conant succeeded Bush as Chairman of the NDRC, which was subsumed into the OSRD. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Adams" title="Roger Adams">Roger Adams</a>, a contemporary of Conant's at Harvard in the 1910s, succeeded him as head of Division B.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart194836.E2.80.9338_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart194836.E2.80.9338-89">[89]</a></sup> Conant became the driving force of the NDRC on personnel and policy matters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart194868_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart194868-90">[90]</a></sup> The NDRC would work hand in hand with the Army and Navy's research efforts, supplementing rather than supplanting them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart19488_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart19488-86">[86]</a></sup> It was specifically charged with investigating <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart19489_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart19489-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A group of in suits men sit around, laughing. On the blackboard behind is a discarded plan for assembling a uranium core." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG/300px-Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG" width="300" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG/450px-Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG/600px-Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2795" data-file-height="2007" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawrence_Compton_Bush_Conant_Compton_Loomis_83d40m_March_1940_meeting_UCB.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conant attending a meeting at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a> in 1940. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Ernest_O._Lawrence" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernest O. Lawrence">Ernest O. Lawrence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_H._Compton" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur H. Compton">Arthur H. Compton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a>, Conant, <a href="/wiki/Karl_T._Compton" class="mw-redirect" title="Karl T. Compton">Karl T. Compton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Lee_Loomis" title="Alfred Lee Loomis">Alfred L. Loomis</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In February 1941, Roosevelt sent Conant to Britain as head of a mission that also included <a href="/wiki/Frederick_L._Hovde" title="Frederick L. Hovde">Frederick L. Hovde</a> from <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carroll_L._Wilson" title="Carroll L. Wilson">Carroll L. Wilson</a> from MIT, to evaluate the research being carried out there and the prospects for cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart1948168.E2.80.93169_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart1948168.E2.80.93169-92">[92]</a></sup> The 1940 <a href="/wiki/Tizard_Mission" title="Tizard Mission">Tizard Mission</a> had revealed that American technology was some years behind that of Britain in many fields, most notably <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, and cooperation was eagerly sought. Conant had lunch with Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lindemann" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Lindemann">Frederick Lindemann</a>, his leading scientific adviser, and an audience with <a href="/wiki/King_George_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="King George VI">King George VI</a> at <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a>. At a subsequent meeting, Lindemann told Conant about British progress towards developing an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a>. What most impressed Conant was the British conviction that it was feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993144.E2.80.93146_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993144.E2.80.93146-93">[93]</a></sup> That the British program was ahead of the American one raised the possibility in Conant's mind that the <a href="/wiki/German_nuclear_energy_project" class="mw-redirect" title="German nuclear energy project">German nuclear energy project</a> might be even further ahead, as Germany was generally acknowledged to be a world leader in nuclear physics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993160.E2.80.93162_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993160.E2.80.93162-94">[94]</a></sup> Later that year, Churchill, as Chancellor of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bristol" title="University of Bristol">University of Bristol</a>, conferred an honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a> degree on Conant <i>in absentia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conant subsequently moved to restrict cooperation with Britain on nuclear energy, particularly its post-war aspects, and became involved in heated negotiations with <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Akers" title="Wallace Akers">Wallace Akers</a>, the representative of <a href="/wiki/Tube_Alloys" title="Tube Alloys">Tube Alloys</a>, the British atomic project.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993173.E2.80.93174_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993173.E2.80.93174-97">[97]</a></sup> Conant's tough stance, under which the British were excluded except where their assistance was vital, resulted in British retaliation, and a complete breakdown of cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993181.E2.80.93187_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993181.E2.80.93187-98">[98]</a></sup> His objections were swept aside by Roosevelt, who brokered the 1943 <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Agreement" title="Quebec Agreement">Quebec Agreement</a> with Churchill, that restored full cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993190.E2.80.93191_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993190.E2.80.93191-99">[99]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Conference,_1943" title="Quebec Conference, 1943">Quebec Conference</a>, Churchill visited Conant at Harvard, where Conant returned the 1941 gesture and presented Churchill with an honorary Doctor of Laws degree.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> After the United States entered the war in December 1941, the OSRD handed the atomic bomb project, better known as the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-101">[101]</a></sup> over to the Army, with Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Leslie_R._Groves" class="mw-redirect" title="Leslie R. Groves">Leslie R. Groves</a> as project director. A meeting that included Conant decided Groves should be answerable to a small committee called the Military Policy Committee, chaired by Bush, with Conant as his alternate. Thus, Conant remained involved in the administration of the Manhattan Project at its highest levels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson196278.E2.80.9383_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson196278.E2.80.9383-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 1942, Roosevelt appointed Conant to the Rubber Survey Committee. Chaired by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_M._Baruch" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard M. Baruch">Bernard M. Baruch</a>, a trusted adviser and confidant of Roosevelt, the committee was tasked with reviewing the synthetic rubber program.<sup id="cite_ref-acs-rubber_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acs-rubber-103">[103]</a></sup> Corporations used <a href="/wiki/Patent_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Patent law">patent laws</a> to restrict competition and stifle innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKevles19776_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKevles19776-104">[104]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Malaya,_North_Borneo_and_Sarawak" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese occupation of Malaya, North Borneo and Sarawak">Japanese occupation of Malaya, North Borneo and Sarawak</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese occupation of Indonesia">Japanese occupation of Indonesia</a>, cut off 90 percent of the supply of natural rubber,<sup id="cite_ref-acs-rubber_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acs-rubber-103">[103]</a></sup> the rubber shortage became a national scandal,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKevles19778_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKevles19778-105">[105]</a></sup> and the development of synthetic substitutes, an urgent priority.<sup id="cite_ref-acs-rubber_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acs-rubber-103">[103]</a></sup> Baruch dealt with the difficult political issues;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970311.E2.80.93317_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970311.E2.80.93317-106">[106]</a></sup> Conant concerned himself with the technical ones. There were a number of different synthetic rubber products to choose from. In addition to <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Neoprene" title="Neoprene">neoprene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_of_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil of New Jersey">Standard Oil</a> had licensed German patents for a <a href="/wiki/Copolymer" title="Copolymer">copolymer</a> called <a href="/wiki/Buna-N" class="mw-redirect" title="Buna-N">Buna-N</a> and a related product, <a href="/wiki/Buna-S" class="mw-redirect" title="Buna-S">Buna-S</a>. None had been manufactured on the scale now required, and there was pressure from agricultural interests to choose a process which involved making raw materials from farm products.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970319.E2.80.93320_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970319.E2.80.93320-107">[107]</a></sup> The Rubber Survey Committee made a series of recommendations, including the appointment of a rubber director, and the construction and operation of 51 factories to supply the materials needed for synthetic rubber production.<sup id="cite_ref-acs-rubber_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acs-rubber-103">[103]</a></sup> Technical problems dogged the program through 1943, but by late 1944 plants were in operation with an annual capacity of over a million tons, most of which was Buna-S.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970327_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970327-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1945, Conant became part of the <a href="/wiki/Interim_Committee" title="Interim Committee">Interim Committee</a> that was formed to advise the new president, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> on nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962344.E2.80.93345_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962344.E2.80.93345-109">[109]</a></sup> The Interim Committee decided that the atomic bomb should be used against an industrial target in Japan as soon as possible and without warning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962360.E2.80.93361_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962360.E2.80.93361-110">[110]</a></sup> On July 16, 1945, Conant was among the dignitaries present at the <a href="/wiki/Holloman_Air_Force_Base" title="Holloman Air Force Base">Alamogordo Bombing and Gunnery Range</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity nuclear test</a>, the first detonation of an atomic bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962378_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962378-111">[111]</a></sup> After the war, Conant became concerned about growing criticism in the United States of the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> by figures like <a href="/wiki/Norman_Cousins" title="Norman Cousins">Norman Cousins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reinhold_Niebuhr" title="Reinhold Niebuhr">Reinhold Niebuhr</a>. He played an important behind-the-scenes role in shaping public opinion by instigating and then editing an influential February 1947 <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's</a></i> article entitled "The Decision to Use the Atomic Bomb". Written by former Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Stimson" title="Henry L. Stimson">Henry L. Stimson</a> with the help of <a href="/wiki/McGeorge_Bundy" title="McGeorge Bundy">McGeorge Bundy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993294.E2.80.93299_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993294.E2.80.93299-112">[112]</a></sup> the article stressed that the atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki were used to avoid the possibility of "over a million casualties",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStimson1947102_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStimson1947102-113">[113]</a></sup> from a figure found in the estimates given to the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> by its Joint Planning Staff in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco1997536.E2.80.93537_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco1997536.E2.80.93537-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War">Cold War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cold War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1946" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1946">Atomic Energy Act of 1946</a> replaced the wartime Manhattan Project with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> (AEC) on January 1, 1947. The Act also established the General Advisory Committee (GAC) within the AEC to provide it with scientific and technical advice. It was widely expected that Conant would chair the GAC, but the position went to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Oppenheimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Oppenheimer">Robert Oppenheimer</a>, the wartime director of the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> that had designed and developed the first atomic bombs. At the same time, the Joint Research and Development Board (JRDC) was established to coordinate defense research, and Bush asked Conant to head its atomic energy subcommittee, on which Oppenheimer also served.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993305.E2.80.93309_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993305.E2.80.93309-115">[115]</a></sup> When the new AEC chairman <a href="/wiki/David_E._Lilienthal" title="David E. Lilienthal">David E. Lilienthal</a> raised security concerns about Oppenheimer's relationships with <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communists</a>, including Oppenheimer's brother <a href="/wiki/Frank_Oppenheimer" title="Frank Oppenheimer">Frank Oppenheimer</a>, his wife Kitty and his former girlfriend <a href="/wiki/Jean_Tatlock" title="Jean Tatlock">Jean Tatlock</a>, Bush and Conant reassured Lilienthal that they had known about it when they had placed Oppenheimer in charge at Los Alamos in 1942. With such expressions of support, AEC issued Oppenheimer a <a href="/wiki/Q_clearance" title="Q clearance">Q clearance</a>, granting him access to atomic secrets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993316.E2.80.93319_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993316.E2.80.93319-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Truman,_Bush_and_Conant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three smiling men in suits" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg/300px-Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg" width="300" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg/450px-Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg/600px-Truman%2C_Bush_and_Conant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="891" data-file-height="664" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Truman,_Bush_and_Conant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Harry S. Truman, center, in 1948 presents Conant, at right, with the civilian <a href="/wiki/Medal_for_Merit" title="Medal for Merit">Medal for Merit</a> award with bronze palm. Vannevar Bush watches at left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By September 1948, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Scare" title="Red Scare">Red Scare</a> began to take hold, and Conant called for a ban on hiring teachers who were communists, although not for the dismissal of those who had already been hired. A debate ensued over whether communist educators could teach apolitical subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993424_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993424-117">[117]</a></sup> Conant was a member of the Educational Policies Commission (EPC), a body to which he had been appointed in 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993430_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993430-118">[118]</a></sup> When it next met in March 1949, Conant's push for a ban was supported by the president of Columbia University, <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="General of the Army">General of the Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>. The two found common ground in their belief in ideology-based education,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993443_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993443-119">[119]</a></sup> which Conant called "democratic education". He did not see public education as a side effect of American democracy, but as one of its principal driving forces,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993401.E2.80.93403_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993401.E2.80.93403-120">[120]</a></sup> and he disapproved of the public funding of <a href="/wiki/Denominational_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Denominational school">denominational schools</a> that he observed in Australia during his visit there in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993586.2C_592_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993586.2C_592-121">[121]</a></sup> He called for increased federal spending on education, and higher taxes to redistribute wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993401.E2.80.93403_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993401.E2.80.93403-120">[120]</a></sup> His thinking was outlined in his books <i>Education in a Divided World</i> in 1948,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993366.E2.80.93367_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993366.E2.80.93367-122">[122]</a></sup> and <i>Education and Liberty</i> in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993587_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993587-123">[123]</a></sup> In 1952, he went further and endorsed the dismissal of academics who invoked the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifth</a> under questioning by the <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Un-American Activities Committee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993625_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993625-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>A sign of Conant's declining influence occurred in 1950, when he was passed over for the post of President of the National Academy of Sciences in favor of <a href="/wiki/Detlev_Bronk" title="Detlev Bronk">Detlev Bronk</a>, the President of <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a>, after a "revolt" by scientists unhappy with Conant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993484.E2.80.93486_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993484.E2.80.93486-125">[125]</a></sup> The GAC was enormously influential throughout the late 1940s, but the opposition of Oppenheimer and Conant to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>, only to be overridden by Truman in 1950, diminished its stature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993482_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993482-126">[126]</a></sup> It was reduced further when Oppenheimer and Conant were not reappointed when their terms expired in 1952, depriving the GAC of its two best-known members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969518.E2.80.93519_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969518.E2.80.93519-127">[127]</a></sup> Conant was appointed to the National Science Board, which administered the new <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>, and was elected its chairman, but this body had little financial or political clout.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969560_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969560-128">[128]</a></sup> In April 1951, Truman appointed Conant to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_President%27s_Council_of_Advisors_on_Science_and_Technology" title="United States President's Council of Advisors on Science and Technology">Science Advisory Committee</a>, but it would not develop into an influential body until the Eisenhower administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993566.E2.80.93567_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993566.E2.80.93567-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conant's experience with the Manhattan Project convinced him that the public needed a better understanding of science, and he moved to revitalize the <a href="/wiki/History_and_philosophy_of_science" title="History and philosophy of science">history and philosophy of science</a> program at Harvard. He took the lead personally by teaching a new undergraduate course, Natural Science 4, "On Understanding Science". His course notes became the basis for a book of the same name, published in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411-130">[130]</a></sup> In 1952, he began teaching another undergraduate course, Philosophy 150, "A Philosophy of Science".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969587_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettDuncan1969587-131">[131]</a></sup> In his teachings and writing on the philosophy of science, he drew heavily on those of his Harvard colleague <a href="/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine" title="Willard Van Orman Quine">Willard Van Orman Quine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiddle2011552_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiddle2011552-132">[132]</a></sup> Conant contributed four chapters to the 1957 <i>Harvard Case Histories in Experimental Science</i>, including an account of the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411-130">[130]</a></sup> In 1951, he published <i>Science and Common Sense</i>, in which he attempted to explain the ways of scientists to laymen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993556.E2.80.93557_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993556.E2.80.93557-133">[133]</a></sup> Conant's ideas about scientific progress would come under attack by his own protÃ©gÃ©s, notably <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuhn" title="Thomas Kuhn">Thomas Kuhn</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_of_Scientific_Revolutions" title="The Structure of Scientific Revolutions">The Structure of Scientific Revolutions</a></i>. Conant commented on Kuhn's manuscript in draft form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993409.E2.80.93411-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Commissioner">High Commissioner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: High Commissioner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="A man poses at a desk. There is an American flag behind him." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg/300px-Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg" width="300" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg/450px-Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg/600px-Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1156" data-file-height="843" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._James_B_Conant_1953_Berlin.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conant as <a href="/wiki/Allied_High_Commission" title="Allied High Commission">United States High Commissioner</a> for Germany, 1953.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 1951 Conant had been approached by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_Acheson" title="Dean Acheson">Dean Acheson</a>, about replacing <a href="/wiki/John_J._McCloy" title="John J. McCloy">John J. McCloy</a> as <a href="/wiki/Allied_High_Commission" title="Allied High Commission">United States High Commissioner</a> for Germany, but had declined. However, after Eisenhower was elected president in 1952, Conant was again offered the job by the new Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a>, and this time he accepted. At the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Board_of_Overseers" title="Harvard Board of Overseers">Harvard Board of Overseers</a> meeting on January 12, 1952, Conant announced that he would retire in September 1953 after twenty years at Harvard, having reached the pension age of sixty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993638.E2.80.93646_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993638.E2.80.93646-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConant1970533.E2.80.93537_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConant1970533.E2.80.93537-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Germany, there were major issues to be decided. Germany was still occupied by the Soviet Union, the United States, Great Britain and France. Dealing with the wartime allies was a major task for the high commissioner. West Germany, made up of the zones occupied by the three western powers, had been granted control of its own affairs, except for defense and foreign policy, in 1949. While most Germans wanted a neutral and reunited Germany, the Eisenhower administration sought to reduce its defense spending by rearming Germany and replacing American troops with Germans. Meanwhile, the House Un-American Activities Committee slammed Conant's staff as communist sympathizers and called for books by communist authors held in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Information_Agency" title="United States Information Agency">United States Information Agency</a> (USIA) libraries in Germany to be burned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993654.E2.80.93657_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993654.E2.80.93657-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first crisis to occur on Conant's watch was the <a href="/wiki/Uprising_of_1953_in_East_Germany" title="Uprising of 1953 in East Germany">uprising of 1953 in East Germany</a>. This brought the reunification issue to the fore. <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer" title="Konrad Adenauer">Konrad Adenauer</a>'s deft handling of the issue enabled him to handily win re-election as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany_(Federal_Republic_of_Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor of Germany (Federal Republic of Germany)">Chancellor of West Germany</a> in September, but this also strengthened his hand in negotiations with Conant. Adenauer did not want his country to become a bargaining chip between the United States and the Soviet Union, nor did he want it to become a nuclear battlefield, a prospect raised by the arrival of American tactical nuclear weapons in 1953 as part of the Eisenhower administration's <a href="/wiki/New_Look_(policy)" title="New Look (policy)">New Look policy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993659.E2.80.93666_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993659.E2.80.93666-137">[137]</a></sup> Conant lobbied for the <a href="/wiki/European_Defense_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Defense Community">European Defense Community</a>, which would have established a <a href="/wiki/Pan-European_military" class="mw-redirect" title="Pan-European military">pan-European military</a>. This seemed to be the only way that German rearmament would be accepted, but opposition from France killed the plan. In what Conant considered a minor miracle, France's actions cleared the way for West Germany to become part of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> with its own army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993685_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993685-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
