<p>At noon on May 6, 1955, Conant, along with the high commissioners from Britain and France, signed the documents ending Allied control of West Germany, admitting it to NATO, and allowing it to rearm. The office of the United States High Commissioner was abolished and Conant became instead the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_West_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Ambassador to West Germany">United States Ambassador to West Germany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993687_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993687-139">[139]</a></sup> His role was now to encourage West Germany to build up its forces, while reassuring the Germans that doing so would not result in a United States withdrawal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993693.E2.80.93694_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993693.E2.80.93694-140">[140]</a></sup> Being fluent in German, Conant was able to give speeches to German audiences. He paid numerous visits to German educational and scientific organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979224_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979224-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Conant returned to the United States in February 1957, and moved to an apartment on the <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">Upper East Side</a> of New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993706.E2.80.93709_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993706.E2.80.93709-142">[142]</a></sup> Between 1957 and 1965, the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Corporation_of_New_York" title="Carnegie Corporation of New York">Carnegie Corporation of New York</a> gave him over a million dollars to write studies of education.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993740_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993740-143">[143]</a></sup> In 1959 he published <i>The American High School Today</i>. This became a best seller, resulting in Conant's appearance on the cover of <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a> on September 14, 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993714_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993714-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In it, Conant called for a number of reforms, including the consolidation of high schools into larger bodies that could offer a broader range of curriculum choices. Although it was slammed by critics of the American system, who hoped for a system of education based on the European model, it did lead to a wave of reforms across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979225_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979225-146">[146]</a></sup> His subsequent <i>Slums and Suburbs</i> in 1961 was far more controversial in its treatment of racial issues. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Desegregation_busing_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Desegregation busing in the United States">busing</a> as impractical, Conant urged Americans "to accept <i>de facto</i> segregated schools".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993726_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993726-147">[147]</a></sup> This did not go over well with civil rights groups, and by 1964 Conant was forced to admit that he had been wrong.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993726_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993726-147">[147]</a></sup> In <i>The Education of American Teachers</i> in 1963, Conant found much to criticize about the training of teachers. Most controversial was his defense of the arrangement by which teachers were certified by independent bodies rather than the teacher training colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979225_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKistiakowskyWestheimer1979225-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> presented Conant with the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, with special distinction, on December 6, 1963. He had been selected for the award by President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, but the ceremony had been delayed, and went ahead after Kennedy's assassination in November 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993735.E2.80.93736_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993735.E2.80.93736-148">[148]</a></sup> In February 1970, President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> presented Conant with the Atomic Pioneers Award from the Atomic Energy Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Other awards that Conant received during his long career included being made a Commander of <a href="/wiki/Legion_d%27Honneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Legion d'Honneur">Legion d'Honneur</a> by France in 1936 and an Honorary <a href="/wiki/Commander_of_the_Order_of_the_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Commander of the Order of the British Empire">Commander of the Order of the British Empire</a> by Britain in 1948, and he was awarded the Grand Cross of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Germany" title="Order of Merit of the Federal Republic of Germany">Order of Merit of the Federal Republic of Germany</a> in 1957. He was also awarded over 50 honorary degrees,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983110.E2.80.93112_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett1983110.E2.80.93112-150">[150]</a></sup> and was posthumously inducted into the Alpha Chi Sigma Hall of Fame in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-alpha_chi_sigma_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alpha_chi_sigma-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1965 and 1969, Conant, suffering from a heart condition, worked on his biography, <i>My Several Lives</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993741.E2.80.93742_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993741.E2.80.93742-151">[151]</a></sup> He became increasingly infirm, and suffered a series of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a> in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993754_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHershberg1993754-152">[152]</a></sup> He died in a nursing home in <a href="/wiki/Hanover,_New_Hampshire" title="Hanover, New Hampshire">Hanover, New Hampshire</a>, on February 11, 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> His body was cremated and his ashes interred in the Thayer-Richards family plot at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Auburn_Cemetery" title="Mount Auburn Cemetery">Mount Auburn Cemetery</a>. He was survived by his wife and sons. His papers are in the Harvard University Archives.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Among them was a sealed brown Manila envelope that Conant had given the archives in 1951, with instructions that it was to be opened by the President of Harvard in the 21st century. It was given to the 28th President of Harvard University, <a href="/wiki/Drew_Faust" class="mw-redirect" title="Drew Faust">Drew Faust</a>, in 2007. She broke the seal, and found that it contained a letter in which Conant expressed his hopes and fears for the future to his successor. "You will receive this note and be in charge of a more prosperous and significant institution than the one over which I have the honor to preside", he wrote. "That [Harvard] will maintain the traditions of academic freedom, of tolerance for heresy, I feel sure."<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Graduate_students">Graduate students</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Bryant_Conant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Graduate students">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Former graduate students of Conant include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Louis_Fieser" title="Louis Fieser">Louis Fieser</a> â organic chemist and professor emeritus at Harvard University renowned as the inventor of a militarily effective form of napalm. His award-winning research included work on blood-clotting agents including the first synthesis of vitamin K, synthesis and screening of quinones as antimalarial drugs, work with steroids leading to the synthesis of cortisone, and study of the nature of polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_S._Garvey" title="Benjamin S. Garvey">Benjamin S. Garvey</a> â noted chemist at BF Goodrich who worked on the development of synthetic rubber, contributed to understanding of vulcanization, and developed early techniques for small scale evaluation of rubbers.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frank_Westheimer" title="Frank Westheimer">Frank Westheimer</a> â was the Morris Loeb Professor of Chemistry Emeritus at Harvard University.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Conant published a series of three papers on the reaction of inorganic iodide with organic halides:</span>
<ul>
